+0000","feedId":13345,"bgimg":"https://chromeunboxed.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/08/ChromeOSAndroidPhoneHubFeature-1024x576.jpg","linkMd5":"47e49612f872e45c644497454c9e9165","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx30/cdn17@2020_2/2020/09/05/08-50-11-737_149ed5ac97852cfa.webp","destWidth":1024,"destHeight":576,"sourceBytes":46378,"destBytes":21404,"author":"Robby Payne","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://chromeunboxed.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/08/ChromeOSAndroidPhoneHubFeature-1024x576.jpg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx30/cdn17@2020_2/2020/09/05/08-50-11-737_149ed5ac97852cfa.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1599295810956},{"createdTime":"2020-09-05 16:50:10","updatedTime":"2020-09-05 16:50:10","title":"Proof that Google may be making a Pixelbook 2 for 2020 after all","link":"https://chromeunboxed.com/?p=24972","description":"<a href=\"https://chromeunboxed.com/google-pixelbook-2-assistant-key-proof-halvor-tiger-lake\" title=\"Proof that Google may be making a Pixelbook 2 for 2020 after all\" rel=\"nofollow\"><img width=\"1024\" height=\"682\" src=\"https://chromeunboxed.com/wp-content/uploads/2019/07/PixelbookSlateFeature-1024x682.jpg\" class=\"webfeedsFeaturedVisual wp-post-image\" alt=\"\" loading=\"lazy\" style=\"display: block; margin: auto; margin-bottom: 10px;max-width: 100%;\" link_thumbnail=\"1\" srcset=\"https://chromeunboxed.com/wp-content/uploads/2019/07/PixelbookSlateFeature-1024x682.jpg 1024w, https://chromeunboxed.com/wp-content/uploads/2019/07/PixelbookSlateFeature-300x200.jpg 300w, https://chromeunboxed.com/wp-content/uploads/2019/07/PixelbookSlateFeature-768x512.jpg 768w, https://chromeunboxed.com/wp-content/uploads/2019/07/PixelbookSlateFeature.jpg 2000w\" sizes=\"(max-width: 1024px) 100vw, 1024px\" data-attachment-id=\"9847\" data-permalink=\"https://chromeunboxed.com/made-by-google-pixelbook-atlas-chromebook-fcc-september-hardware-event/pixelbookslatefeature/\" data-orig-file=\"https://chromeunboxed.com/wp-content/uploads/2019/07/PixelbookSlateFeature.jpg\" data-orig-size=\"2000,1333\" data-comments-opened=\"1\" data-image-meta=\"{&quot;aperture&quot;:&quot;0&quot;,&quot;credit&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;camera&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;caption&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;created_timestamp&quot;:&quot;0&quot;,&quot;copyright&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;focal_length&quot;:&quot;0&quot;,&quot;iso&quot;:&quot;0&quot;,&quot;shutter_speed&quot;:&quot;0&quot;,&quot;title&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;orientation&quot;:&quot;0&quot;}\" data-image-title=\"PixelbookSlateFeature\" data-image-description=\"\" data-medium-file=\"https://chromeunboxed.com/wp-content/uploads/2019/07/PixelbookSlateFeature-300x200.jpg\" data-large-file=\"https://chromeunboxed.com/wp-content/uploads/2019/07/PixelbookSlateFeature-1024x682.jpg\" /></a> \n<p>At this point in 2020, <a rel=\"noreferrer noopener\" href=\"https://chromeunboxed.com/chromebooks-upcoming-development-boards-devices-chrome-os/\" data-type=\"post\" data-id=\"18348\" target=\"_blank\">we have a lot of Chromebooks in different development phases.</a> From a slew of ‘Hatch’ (10th-gen Intel) Chromebooks yet to launch to many more MediaTek-powered devices like the Lenovo Chromebook Duet to the AMD-powered ‘Zork’ devices and more, there are enough new Chromebooks on the horizon to make any Chrome OS fan’s head spin. Without question, there are more upcoming devices in development in the Chromebook world right now than there ever have been at any point in history.</p> \n<p>Yet, there’s one Chromebook many of us have been on the hunt for week after week that has yet to show itself: <strong>the next #madebyGoogle Chromebook.</strong> Not long ago, I’d even conceded the fact that <a rel=\"noreferrer noopener\" href=\"https://chromeunboxed.com/google-pixelbook-2-not-happening-soon/\" data-type=\"post\" data-id=\"20097\" target=\"_blank\">Google may not be making a new Chromebook in-house for the </a><a href=\"https://chromeunboxed.com/google-pixelbook-2-not-happening-soon/\" data-type=\"post\" data-id=\"20097\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noreferrer noopener\">foreseeable</a><a rel=\"noreferrer noopener\" href=\"https://chromeunboxed.com/google-pixelbook-2-not-happening-soon/\" data-type=\"post\" data-id=\"20097\" target=\"_blank\"> future.</a> When you look at how the ecosystem is thriving right now, who could blame them if they just sat out for a year or two? After all, if Google’s intention was to ultimately set the bar for Chromebook manufacturers with the Pixelbook, Pixel Slate and Pixelbook Go, <strong>I think we’d all agree that their mission is accomplished at this point.</strong></p> \n<p>While I think all of that is true, as a big fan of Google’s hardware, <strong>I simply don’t care.</strong> I don’t care if Google did their part and put manufacturers on a good trajectory. I don’t care if Google accomplished what they set out to do. I don’t care if there isn’t a market need for a new Pixelbook: the fact of the matter is <strong>I want one.</strong> And I’m not alone, here. I see comments and receive emails constantly about users who want a new Chromebook and want one that is designed by Google. That’s not to throw shade on other Chromebooks. I love the Acer Spin 713 I’m typing this on right now, but <strong>it’s no Pixelbook. Nothing quite is.</strong></p> \n<p>There’s something that’s just inherently better about Google’s own Chromebooks: an attention to detail, a quality of build, and a thoughtfulness of design that not only is attractive at launch, but stands the test of time. Take a look at a Pixelbook the next time you see one and <strong>tell me another 3-year-old laptop that looks as distinctive yet is still just as aesthetically pleasing as it was on day one.</strong> I’ll save you some time: there’s nothing quite like it. Even the 7-year-old Chromebook Pixel retains an air of modernity few laptops can boast from that time. The current Pixelbook Go is designed in the same vein and every time I pick it up, I have an impulsive desire to just return to it again because of the way it looks and feels. Every time.</p> \n<h2>Proof another Pixelbook may be coming this year</h2> \n<p>Some of you may think I’m crazy, but if you’ve fallen in love with a Google-made Chromebook, you know exactly what I’m talking about and <strong>you should be as excited as I am about the fact that we have some pretty solid proof that a new Google Chromebook is in the works</strong> and could realistically be launched by the end of this year just like the past 3 in-house Google laptops before it.</p> \n<p>Say hello again to ‘Halvor’ and if – like me – <a href=\"https://chromeunboxed.com/3-new-chromebooks-development-boards-tiger-lake-jasper-lake-intel/\" data-type=\"post\" data-id=\"19886\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noreferrer noopener\">you brushed by this baseboard when we originally reported on it,</a> don’t beat yourself up. You see, ‘Halvor’ is one of an absolute gaggle of new Chromebooks coming that is based on the ‘Volteer’ baseboard. ‘Volteer’ is the main board that is being used to usher in <a href=\"https://chromeunboxed.com/chrome-os-begins-prepping-for-intels-10nm-tiger-lake-cpu/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noreferrer noopener\">the era of Intel Tiger Lake Chromebooks.</a> These devices will have things like <a href=\"https://chromeunboxed.com/chromebooks-graphics-gpu-intel-tiger-lake-powerful/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noreferrer noopener\">Intel’s new Xe integrated graphics,</a> <a href=\"https://chromeunboxed.com/chrome-os-begins-prepping-for-intels-10nm-tiger-lake-cpu/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noreferrer noopener\">a 10nm process for faster speeds and improved battery life,</a> <a href=\"https://chromeunboxed.com/chromebooks-4k-tiger-lake-volteer-development/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noreferrer noopener\">support for 4K screens,</a> <a href=\"https://chromeunboxed.com/chromebooks-webcam-1080p-hdr-low-light-volteer-tiger-lake-intel/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noreferrer noopener\">better webcams,</a> and <a href=\"https://chromeunboxed.com/chrome-os-prepares-to-embrace-usb-4-0/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noreferrer noopener\">USB 4.</a> Overall, they will be a fantastic update to the current Chromebooks being launched in 2020 so far and will be the next generation that will carry the flagship banner for Chrome OS into 2021.</p> \n<p><strong>Now that we have re-introductions covered, let’s get to the evidence.</strong> Tonight as I was browsing through the Chromium Repositories, I came across a commit that contained the message I’ve been seeking for months at this point: <strong>a message about the Assistant key.</strong> You see, the only Chromebooks ever made with the dedicated Assistant key up to this point are all Google-made devices. The Pixelbook began the trend in 2017, followed by the Pixel Slate in 2018 and the Pixelbook Go in 2019. Sure, there are a couple Chrome OS keyboards with this special key (Brydge and Logitech make them), but <strong>no Chromebooks other than Google’s own possess it. ‘Halvor’ is the lone exception:</strong></p> \n<blockquote class=\"wp-block-quote\">\n <p>Volteer: add support for ASSISTANT key on Halvor </p>\n <p>Halvor uses the METAL DOME keyboard, which supports ASSISTANT key. </p>\n <p>BUG=<a rel=\"noreferrer noopener\" href=\"https://issuetracker.google.com/166194260\" target=\"_blank\">b:166194260</a> TEST=scp 81-halvor-keyboard.hwdb into /etc/udev/hwdb.d udevadm hwdb –update reboot DUT, then to launch Google Assistant with ASSISTANT key</p>\n <cite><a href=\"https://chromeunboxed.com/?p=24972/166194260\nTEST=scp 81-halvor-keyboard.hwdb into /etc/udev/hwdb.d\n     udevadm hwdb --update\n     reboot DUT, then to luanch Google Assistant with ASSISTANT ke\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noreferrer noopener\">via the Chromium Gerrit</a></cite>\n</blockquote> \n<p>Add to this commit the fact that this change is being made for only one ‘Volteer’ device in ‘Halvor’ instead of across the board for all the ‘Volteer’ derivatives and you have some pretty good evidence that we’re looking at Google’s next Chromebook. We thought at one time we’d see all Chromebooks begin shipping with an Assistant key on the keyboard, but that has simply not been the case. For whatever reason, Google has always kept this to themselves and it is this fact that makes us feel very confident that ‘Halvor’ is a <strong>#madebyGoogle</strong> Chromebook.</p> \n<h2>The ‘Halvor’ timeline stacks up, too</h2> \n<p>There are a few more points that line up very well with a Pixelbook storyline for ‘Halvor’ if you look a bit deeper. First up is the overall timeline. With the Assistant key addition being added just a couple days ago, I was a bit dubious that this device could possibly show up in the fall like other Pixelbooks have. However, when you look at the timelines of other Google Chromebooks, ‘Halvor’ has had plenty of time for development, <a href=\"https://chromium-review.googlesource.com/c/chromiumos/third_party/coreboot/+/2126765\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noreferrer noopener\">beginning it’s life in March of 2020</a> and being based on ‘Volteer’ that began all the way back in September of 2019.</p> \n<div class=\"crp_related crp_related_block  \">\n <h5>more from Chrome Unboxed</h5>\n <ul>\n  <li><a href=\"https://chromeunboxed.com/chromebooks-upcoming-development-boards-devices-chrome-os/\" target=\"_blank\" class=\"post-18348\"><img loading=\"lazy\" width=\"150\" height=\"150\" src=\"https://chromeunboxed.com/wp-content/uploads/2019/12/NewWaveChromebooksEarly2020-150x150.jpg\" class=\"crp_thumb crp_featured\" alt=\"All the upcoming Chrome OS devices and boards we're tracking right now [UPDATE: 7/9/2020]\" title=\"All the upcoming Chrome OS devices and boards we're tracking right now [UPDATE: 7/9/2020]\" /><span class=\"crp_title\">All the upcoming Chrome OS devices and boards we’re tracking right now [UPDATE: 7/9/2020]</span></a><span class=\"crp_author\"> by <a href=\"https://chromeunboxed.com/author/robbypayne/\">Robby Payne</a></span> </li>\n </ul>\n <div class=\"crp_clear\"></div>\n</div> \n<p>For reference, the <a rel=\"noreferrer noopener\" href=\"https://chromium-review.googlesource.com/c/chromiumos/chromite/+/1014264\" target=\"_blank\">Pixel Slate began life in April of 2018</a> and launched in the fall of that year. For a device on a fresh, new baseboard (the Pixelbook Go would ride that same board into 2019) to start in April and ship in November is pretty impressive, honestly. The Slate was no standard device, either, and had to clear the hurdles of a new display, a new form factor, and a custom keyboard solution. <strong>Come to think of it, that’s probably why it’s launch was a bit of a mess.</strong></p> \n<p>Going back to the original Pixelbook, the <a href=\"https://chromium-review.googlesource.com/c/chromiumos/overlays/board-overlays/+/544794\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noreferrer noopener\">Assistant key was added to it in late June the year it was launched.</a> Back then, the Assistant on a Chromebook wasn’t even a thing, so the fact that Google added the key to the keyboard only 3 months before they announced the Pixelbook tells me that this step is not essential to the overall development cycle of a device. After all, the difference in timing on the Pixelbook and ‘Halvor’ for adding the Assistant key is only two months. <strong>With the Assistant being settled in on Chrome OS at this point, a later addition isn’t actually surprising at all.</strong></p> \n<p>Finally, a Tiger Lake Chromebook is a logical fit for the next Pixelbook and why<strong> I think we could be looking at a Pixelbook 2 instead of something like a Pixelbook Go. </strong> The Pixelbook Go launched with a processor that was over a year old at the time to keep pricing down, so the fact that ‘Halvor’ is being built with the best available silicon means Google is likely making a flagship device this time around. Users have been clamoring for a proper follow-up to the original Pixelbook for quite some time now, so this move would make massive amounts of sense for Google at this stage.</p> \n<p><a href=\"https://chromeunboxed.com/the-chrome-cast-episode-69-interview-john-maletis-chrome-os-head-ux/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noreferrer noopener\">With Chromebook adoption through the roof in 2020</a> and the platform growing in every possible way, <strong>the Pixelbook 2 could be Google’s first truly breakout Chromebook made and designed by them. </strong> The Chromebook Pixels (2013 &amp; 2015), the Pixelbook and the Pixel Slate were too costly at a time when broader Chromebook adoption wasn’t anything like what it is now. A well-made, high-spec, Google-y Chromebook launched before the end of the year could finally deliver the mass-appeal Chromebook from Google we’ve all been waiting for. Now that we know ‘Halvor’ may be that Chromebook, we’ll be keeping a keen eye out for any further clues we can find.</p> \n<p><a href=\"https://chrmshop.com/\" target=\"_blank\" class=\"BuyButton\" 16:50:10","updatedTime":"2020-09-05 16:50:10","title":"Developers working on a “Diagnostics App” for Chrome OS","link":"https://chromeunboxed.com/?p=24950","description":"<a srcset=\"https://chromeunboxed.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/08/chromebook-diagnostic-app-1024x576.jpg 1024w, https://chromeunboxed.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/08/chromebook-diagnostic-app-300x169.jpg 1536w, https://chromeunboxed.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/08/chromebook-diagnostic-app.jpg 1600w\" sizes=\"(max-width: 1024px) 100vw, 1024px\" data-attachment-id=\"24966\" data-permalink=\"https://chromeunboxed.com/developers-working-on-a-diagnostics-app-for-chrome-os/chromebook-diagnostic-app/\" data-orig-file=\"https://chromeunboxed.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/08/chromebook-diagnostic-app.jpg\" data-orig-size=\"1600,900\" data-comments-opened=\"1\" data-image-meta=\"{&#34;aperture&#34;:&#34;0&#34;,&#34;credit&#34;:&#34;&#34;,&#34;camera&#34;:&#34;&#34;,&#34;caption&#34;:&#34;&#34;,&#34;created_timestamp&#34;:&#34;0&#34;,&#34;copyright&#34;:&#34;&#34;,&#34;focal_length&#34;:&#34;0&#34;,&#34;iso&#34;:&#34;0&#34;,&#34;shutter_speed&#34;:&#34;0&#34;,&#34;title&#34;:&#34;&#34;,&#34;orientation&#34;:&#34;0&#34;}\" data-image-title=\"chromebook-diagnostic-app\" data-image-description=\"\" data-medium-file=\"https://chromeunboxed.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/08/chromebook-diagnostic-app-300x169.jpg\" data-large-file=\"https://chromeunboxed.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/08/chromebook-diagnostic-app-1024x576.jpg\" /></a>\n<p>Diagnosing system health on Chrome OS may get a little easier with a new update that could be headed our way. As it sits, getting support for your device boils down to one of two worthwhile methods. The first method is to head over to the <a href=\"https://chromeunboxed.com/news/googles-chromebook-help-forum-gets-a-fresh-look/\" data-type=\"post\" data-id=\"6543\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noreferrer noopener\">official Chromebook Support Foru</a>m to see if the community of well-versed volunteers can assist you with your particular Chrome OS issue. The alternative is to use the in-built Chrome OS feedback option. You can use this method by pressing Alt+Shift+i on your keyboard and describing, in detail, the problem you&#8217;re having with your device. When you send your feedback, it will travel to developers with any necessary system information, diagnostics and screenshots, if you include them. </p>\n\n\n\n<p>The problem with submitting feedback is that you aren&#8217;t going receive any direct assistance with your problem. The feedback option is geared specifically for giving developers information needed to address and fix bugs in the Chrome OS software. So, don&#8217;t expect an email back if you&#8217;re simply trying to figure out why you Chromebook won&#8217;t find your new printer you just set up. That&#8217;s not what feedback is for. For those kind of issues, you&#8217;ll need to refer to the aforementioned support forum or perhaps a site like ours. </p>\n\n\n<div class=\"crp_related crp_related_block  \"><h5>more from Chrome Unboxed</h5><ul><li><a href=\"https://chromeunboxed.com/5-keyboard-shortcuts-to-chromebook-like-a-pro/\"    target=\"_blank\"  class=\"post-19087\"><img loading=\"lazy\"  width=\"150\" height=\"150\"  src=\"https://chromeunboxed.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/05/dellchromeoskeyboard-150x150.jpg\" class=\"crp_thumb crp_featured\" alt=\"5 keyboard shortcuts to Chromebook like a pro\" title=\"5 keyboard shortcuts to Chromebook like a pro\" /><span class=\"crp_title\">5 keyboard shortcuts to Chromebook like a pro</span></a><span class=\"crp_author\">  by <a href=\"https://chromeunboxed.com/author/gabe/\">Gabriel Brangers</a></span> </li></ul><div class=\"crp_clear\"></div></div>\n\n\n<p>For many of us that grew up on &#8220;other&#8221; operating systems, we&#8217;re accustomed to a certain level built in diagnostics to aide and assist in pinpointing technical issues. Chrome OS has a &#8220;<a rel=\"noreferrer noopener\" href=\"https://chromeunboxed.com/chromebook-task-manager-ctrlaltdel-sort-of/\" data-type=\"post\" data-id=\"2393\" target=\"_blank\">task manager</a>&#8221; but apart from being able to identify and terminate processes, it does little else. A new commit in the Chromium repository is looking to change that fact. The feature flag hit my device today after an update to Chrome OS 87 in the Canary channel and is listed simply as &#8220;Diagnostic App.&#8221; <strong>So, what does it do?</strong> For now, nothing. It is, as the commit states, a skeleton framework for the system web app that will come along in a future update. However, the description of the Diagnostic App gives us a good idea of what it will do. </p>\n\n\n\n<blockquote class=\"wp-block-quote\"><p>Enables the Diagnostics app that allows Chrome OS users to be able to <br>view their system telemetric information and run diagnostic tests for <br>their device.</p><cite><a href=\"https://chromium-review.googlesource.com/c/chromium/src/+/2368102/4/chrome/browser/flag_descriptions.cc\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noreferrer noopener\">Diagnostic App description</a></cite></blockquote>\n\n\n\n<p>Unlike the Chrome OS task manager, the Diagnostic App will allow users to view system information as well as run specific diagnostic tests that have yet to be identified. There is mention of battery health in one of the commits. There could very well be a test to replace the little-known battery test that lives inside the crosh terminal that outputs battery health as a percentage along with discharge rate and current battery level. A system web application for device diagnostics would be a much more user-friendly and visible way to get these sort of tools in the hands of general consumers. I would presume that this new app will also give users device-specific information that is currently difficult to find in the front-facing UI of Chrome OS. It would be great if the Diagnostic App shows real-time resource usage and system temperatures like the third-party Chrome app Cog. For now, we can only speculate as to what all the Diagnostic App will do but you can bet I&#8217;ll be checking with every update to the Canary channel. </p>\n\n\n\n<div 16:50:10","updatedTime":"2020-09-05 16:50:10","title":"‘Limozeen’ Chromebook begins development, proves Snapdragon Chromebooks are still coming","link":"https://chromeunboxed.com/?p=25207","description":"<a href=\"https://chromeunboxed.com/snapdragon-7c-chromebook-limozeen-trogdor\" title=\"&#8216;Limozeen&#8217; Chromebook begins development, proves Snapdragon Chromebooks are still coming\" rel=\"nofollow\"><img width=\"1024\" height=\"589\" src=\"https://chromeunboxed.com/wp-content/uploads/2019/07/snapdragonChromebooksTrogdor-1024x589.jpg\" class=\"webfeedsFeaturedVisual wp-post-image\" alt=\"\" loading=\"lazy\" style=\"display: block; margin: auto; margin-bottom: 10px;max-width: 100%;\" link_thumbnail=\"1\" srcset=\"https://chromeunboxed.com/wp-content/uploads/2019/07/snapdragonChromebooksTrogdor-1024x589.jpg 1024w, data-orig-file=\"https://chromeunboxed.com/wp-content/uploads/2019/07/snapdragonChromebooksTrogdor.jpg\" data-orig-size=\"2000,1150\" data-comments-opened=\"1\" data-image-meta=\"{&#34;aperture&#34;:&#34;0&#34;,&#34;credit&#34;:&#34;&#34;,&#34;camera&#34;:&#34;&#34;,&#34;caption&#34;:&#34;&#34;,&#34;created_timestamp&#34;:&#34;0&#34;,&#34;copyright&#34;:&#34;&#34;,&#34;focal_length&#34;:&#34;0&#34;,&#34;iso&#34;:&#34;0&#34;,&#34;shutter_speed&#34;:&#34;0&#34;,&#34;title&#34;:&#34;&#34;,&#34;orientation&#34;:&#34;0&#34;}\" data-image-title=\"snapdragonChromebooksTrogdor\" data-image-description=\"\" data-medium-file=\"https://chromeunboxed.com/wp-content/uploads/2019/07/snapdragonChromebooksTrogdor-300x173.jpg\" data-large-file=\"https://chromeunboxed.com/wp-content/uploads/2019/07/snapdragonChromebooksTrogdor-1024x589.jpg\" /></a>\n<p>It&#8217;s been a minute since we found a new Qualcomm Snapdragon-powered Chromebook beginning development.  For those unaware, <a href=\"http://cheza\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noreferrer noopener\">we&#8217;ve had eyes on a Qualcomm Chromebook for quite a long time at this point.</a>  Originally started with the &#8216;Cheza&#8217; baseboard and based on the Snapdragon 845, the idea of a Chromebook with a Snapdragon processor inside has gone through a few changes over the past couple years as we&#8217;ve all been eagerly awaiting the actual product.</p>\n\n\n\n<p>While we don&#8217;t beleive the Snapdragon 845 chip is in play any longer, much of what the Chrome OS team developed for that chip has been ported over to what we are tracking now in the SC7180 that <a rel=\"noreferrer noopener\" href=\"https://chromeunboxed.com/qualcomm-snapdragon-7c-chromebook/\" target=\"_blank\">we feel quite confidently is the new Snapdragon 7c Compute Platform Qualcomm</a> announced in December of 2019.  <a rel=\"noreferrer noopener\" href=\"https://www.qualcomm.com/products/snapdragon-7c-compute-platform\" target=\"_blank\">This chipset</a> makes far more sense for Chromebooks when compared with the phone-focused Snapdragon 845, and since that time, we&#8217;ve uncovered a few more Chromebook development boards that have this new 7c chip inside: &#8216;<a rel=\"noreferrer noopener\" href=\"https://chromeunboxed.com/chromebooks-upcoming-development-boards-devices-chrome-os/\" data-type=\"post\" data-id=\"18348\" target=\"_blank\">Trogdor&#8217;, &#8216;Bubs&#8217;, &#8216;Lazor&#8217;, and &#8216;Pompom&#8217;</a> are the current lineup we&#8217;ve had in front of us for the majority of 2020.  There&#8217;s been movement and development happening, but when compared with the <a rel=\"noreferrer noopener\" href=\"https://chromeunboxed.com/chromebooks-upcoming-development-boards-devices-chrome-os/\" data-type=\"post\" data-id=\"18348\" target=\"_blank\">exploding number of Tiger Lake development devices</a> and AMD-powered boards we are currently tracking, <strong>it began feeling a bit like the Qualcomm Chromebook experiment was stagnating.</strong></p>\n\n\n\n<h2>Meet the newest Qualcomm-powered Chromebook: &#8216;Limozeen&#8217;</h2>\n\n\n\n<p>Just a few days ago, <a rel=\"noreferrer noopener\" href=\"https://chromium-review.googlesource.com/c/chromiumos/platform/ec/+/2385019\" target=\"_blank\">a new developement board was created under the name of &#8216;Limozeen&#8217;</a> and was a direct copy of &#8216;Lazor&#8217; from the ground up.  This means it will posses the same Snapdragon 7c chip and, from the looks of one of only 3 commits available for this device, <a rel=\"noreferrer noopener\" href=\"https://chromium-review.googlesource.com/c/chromiumos/platform/ec/+/2385020\" target=\"_blank\">it will likely be a convertible and have a backlit keyboard.</a></p>\n\n\n<div class=\"crp_related crp_related_block  \"><h5>more from Chrome Unboxed</h5><ul><li><a href=\"https://chromeunboxed.com/qualcomm-snapdragon-7c-chromebook/\"    target=\"_blank\"  class=\"post-13421\"><img loading=\"lazy\"  width=\"150\" height=\"150\"  src=\"https://chromeunboxed.com/wp-content/uploads/2019/08/qualcommChromebookBubs-150x150.jpg\" class=\"crp_thumb crp_featured\" alt=\"Why we feel confident a Qualcomm Snapdragon 7c Chromebook is in development\" title=\"Why we feel confident a Qualcomm Snapdragon 7c Chromebook is in development\" /><span class=\"crp_title\">Why we feel confident a Qualcomm Snapdragon 7c Chromebook is in development</span></a><span class=\"crp_author\">  by <a href=\"https://chromeunboxed.com/author/robbypayne/\">Robby Payne</a></span> </li></ul><div class=\"crp_clear\"></div></div>\n\n\n<p><strong>The truth is, we don&#8217;t know a ton about most of these Qualcomm-powered Chromebooks at the moment.</strong>  What is more important right now is the fact that new boards are being put into play and that signals the fact that more manufacturers are jumping on board to make Chromebooks with Qualcomm silicon inside.  Assuming it will be on par with something like the Qualcomm 765 we&#8217;re seeing in phones like the OnePlus Nord, the upcoming Google Pixel 4a 5G and Pixel 5, <strong>this chip will outperform all the other ARM-based options for Chromebooks currently available.</strong>  <em>It just needs to get here.</em></p>\n\n\n\n<p>While the entire Snapdragon-on-Chromebooks effort has been going on long enough to warrant a 2020 release, I still don&#8217;t feel like that is in the cards at this point.  We&#8217;re unsure what is taking so long, but my hope is an announcement of some sort happens sooner than later.  Just like with the aging &#8216;Cheza&#8217; board and its now 2-year-old 845 chip, these Chromebooks need to get released before they become obsolete.  If delays keep pushing these devices back, they&#8217;ll feel out of date by the time they actually arrive, and that&#8217;s never a good look.  Perhaps an announcement at Qualcomm&#8217;s yearly summit in December will bring Chromebooks into the fold in an official capacity and we&#8217;ll see something &#8211; virtually &#8211; from CES 2021.</p>\n\n\n\n<img src=\"https://chromeunboxed.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/09/patreonInContentDesktop.jpg\" class=\"patreonDesktop\">\n\n\n\n<div class=\"newsletterSignupBlock\">\n\tGet Alerted the swing of things and that means back to schools shopping. While shopping for a Chromebook is a massive undertaking thanks to high demand and short supply, there are still devices to be found and you can save some cash if you shop retailers that offer student and education discounts. This can be a big money saver if you&#8217;re in the market for supplies, PC accessories and gear to get you or your student on their way back to the classroom. We&#8217;ve rounded up some retailers that offer student and teacher discounts when you verify your affiliation with a qualifying EDU institution. </p>\n\n\n\n<h2>Best Buy</h2>\n\n\n\n<p>Best Buy&#8217;s student deals program is very well-known and easy to apply for if you have a student email. With the program, students have access to exclusive savings on a wide variety of products including Chromebooks, gear and accessories. The Student Deals page updates frequently and you can score savings year round. The best part? You can use your current Best Buy account and get verified for Student Deals with your EDU email address. Sign up for Best Buy Student Deals at the link below. </p>\n\n\n\n<p><a rel=\"nofollow noopener noreferrer\" href=\"https://fave.co/2y76Qva\" class=\"BuyButton\" target=\"_blank\">Best Buy Student Deals</a></p>\n\n\n\n\n<h2>Samsung</h2>\n\n\n\n<p>I wasn&#8217;t aware, until recently, that Samsung had a discount program but they do and the savings are huge if you&#8217;re a teacher, student, first responders, military and more. Qualifying shoppers can grab as much as 30% off when you shop on Samsung.com. With the current sale on the Galaxy Chromebook, a student discount would save you an extra $119.85 when you buy the ultra-premium 10th Gen Intel convertible and that&#8217;s on top of the $200 that Samsung already took off of the MSRP. The 30% savings applies to most products from Samsung and shoppers can also add an additional 10% discount to many sale items. Check out the program below. </p>\n\n\n\n<p><a rel=\"nofollow noopener noreferrer\" href=\"https://fave.co/2E4veUc\" class=\"BuyButton\" target=\"_blank\">Samsung Discount Program</a></p>\n\n\n\n<div class=\"crp_related crp_related_block  \"><h5>more from Chrome Unboxed</h5><ul><li><a href=\"https://chromeunboxed.com/chromebooks-hard-to-find-where-to-buy-deals/\"    target=\"_blank\"  class=\"post-17098\"><img loading=\"lazy\"  width=\"150\" height=\"150\"  src=\"https://chromeunboxed.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/04/nochromebooks-150x150.jpg\" class=\"crp_thumb crp_featured\" alt=\"[Update] It&#039;s not you, Chromebooks are getting hard to find\" title=\"[Update] It&#039;s not you, Chromebooks are getting hard to find\" /><span class=\"crp_title\">[Update] It&#8217;s not you, Chromebooks are getting hard to find</span></a><span class=\"crp_author\">  by <a href=\"https://chromeunboxed.com/author/gabe/\">Gabriel Brangers</a></span> </li></ul><div class=\"crp_clear\"></div></div>\n\n\n<h2>Lenovo</h2>\n\n\n\n<p>Lenovo could honestly be your one-stop-shop for everything tech and gear-related if you wanted to get all your shopping done in one fell swoop. Sadly, the PC giant is suffering a massive shortage on Chromebooks but that doesn&#8217;t mean that there isn&#8217;t money to be saved. Students and teachers can sign up and complete a quick verification via ID.me and BOOM, instantly get a site-wide 8% discount on anything and everything Lenovo offers. </p>\n\n\n\n<p>You&#8217;ll find a lot more than just PCs on Lenovo&#8217;s site. The offer Assistant-enabled smart displays, backpacks, PC accessories and you can even grab cool tech like a new <a rel=\"noreferrer noopener\" href=\"https://fave.co/3lvepDs\" target=\"_blank\">FitBit</a> or an <a rel=\"noreferrer noopener\" href=\"https://fave.co/32quAZX\" target=\"_blank\">Xbox One</a>. On top of the student discounts, you can get cash back every day on Lenovo.com when you <a rel=\"noreferrer noopener\" href=\"https://www.rakuten.com/r/GBRANG2?utm_campaign=RAF&#38;utm_medium=RAF%20share&#38;eeid=45830\" target=\"_blank\">shop with Rakuten</a>. That cash adds up quick when you&#8217;re shopping for tech. </p>\n\n\n\n<p><a rel=\"nofollow noopener noreferrer\" href=\"https://fave.co/2CYwDeW\" class=\"BuyButton\" target=\"_blank\">Lenovo Student Discount</a></p>\n\n\n\n\n<h2>Student Beans</h2>\n\n\n\n<p>Speaking of one-stop-shops, Student Beans if probably the most well-known place to go when you&#8217;re looking for student deals. The student discount platform partners with a number of retailers to bring discounts directly to shoppers with a verified account. One such retailer happens to be Google where you can save up to 10% on select hardware from the fine folks in Mountain View. Student Beans also features its own storefront where you can shop all of the exclusive deals when you verify your EDU eligibility. You&#8217;ll find savings such as 35% off select HP products, 10% off at Acer.com and so much more. </p>\n\n\n\n<p>Student Beans offers discounts on so much more than just tech as well. If you have a student heading off to college, Student Beans is a must-have tool in your arsenal of money-saving tricks. Kid Space by Google offers an integrated hub where kids can explore and discover new content to help them learn and create. We&#8217;ll cover more on that later. The more-relevant announcement from Lenovo was the addition of a new Google Assistant speaker to the company&#8217;s growing line of smart-home devices. </p>\n\n\n\n<p>As some of you may know, Lenovo&#8217;s current Smart Clock with the Google Assistant is my favorite smart display with their 7&#8243; full-featured model coming in as a close second. The 4&#8243; bedside clock is the perfect marriage of alarm clock and smart display. You don&#8217;t get video playback or a camera but I don&#8217;t want those features on my nightstand so it&#8217;s perfect. The latest addition hones down the nightstand experience to an even smaller device called the Lenovo Smart Clock Essential.</p>\n\n\n\n<blockquote class=\"wp-block-quote\"><p>Lenovo is once again Clock Essential features the same 1.5&#8243; 3W speaker found in the larger clock from Lenovo which means it should be a decent-sounding device. I&#8217;d put my current one up against the Nest Mini any day of the week. Like its larger sibling, the Essential Smart Clock features a USB port on the back for charging your phone or whatever other accessory you have sitting around. I use it to charge my <a rel=\"noreferrer noopener\" href=\"https://chromeunboxed.com/ticwatch-pro-lte-review-the-wearos-smartwatch-for-the-masses-video/\" data-type=\"post\" data-id=\"14708\" target=\"_blank\">Tic Watch Pro</a> and it works perfectly. The smaller display foregoes a touchscreen and instead, offers a true alarm clock style interface. </p>\n\n\n\n<p>Like the 4&#8243; Smart Clock, the Essential features soft touch volume buttons on the top of the fabric-encased chassis but this model adds a dedicated alarm button and a play/pause button. Lenovo has also added a &#8220;night light&#8221; to this model to help you navigate your bedroom when you have to get up in the middle of the night. I&#8217;m not sure if this will simply be a sensor-activated brightening of the display or if it actually has an in-built light. You&#8217;ll also get a button on the back for muting the microphone which should be standard on any smart speaker. Like any Assistant speaker, you&#8217;ll be able to control all of your connected things from the Essential and it even has a weather widget on the display so you can keep an eye on the temperature. </p>\n\n\n\n<p>The Lenovo Smart Clock Essential goes on sale next month and will retail for $49.99. That means we&#8217;ll likely see some really great prices come the holiday shopping season. In the mean time, the larger Smart Clock happens to be on sale at Best Buy right now for the same $49.99. While I definitely see the appeal of this smaller, more discrete model, $50 for the current Smart Clock is a solid deal and still my recommendation. </p>\n\n\n\n<p><a rel=\"nofollow noopener noreferrer\" href=\"https://fave.co/30mnUtm\" class=\"BuyButton\" target=\"_blank\">4&#8243; Lenovo Smart Clock at Best Buy</a></p>\n\n\n\n<div class=\"newsletterSignupBlock\">\n\tGet Alerted About href=\"https://chromeunboxed.com/backup-and-restore-your-chromebooks-linux-setup/\" title=\"Backup and restore your Chromebook&#8217;s Linux setup\" rel=\"nofollow\"><img width=\"1024\" height=\"576\" src=\"https://chromeunboxed.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/08/linux-backup-restore-chromebook-1024x576.jpg\" class=\"webfeedsFeaturedVisual wp-post-image\" alt=\"\" loading=\"lazy\" style=\"display: block; margin: auto; margin-bottom: 10px;max-width: 100%;\" link_thumbnail=\"1\" srcset=\"https://chromeunboxed.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/08/linux-backup-restore-chromebook-1024x576.jpg 1024w, https://chromeunboxed.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/08/linux-backup-restore-chromebook-300x169.jpg 300w, https://chromeunboxed.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/08/linux-backup-restore-chromebook-768x432.jpg 768w, https://chromeunboxed.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/08/linux-backup-restore-chromebook-1536x864.jpg 1536w, https://chromeunboxed.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/08/linux-backup-restore-chromebook.jpg 1600w\" sizes=\"(max-width: 1024px) 100vw, 1024px\" data-attachment-id=\"25048\" data-permalink=\"https://chromeunboxed.com/backup-and-restore-your-chromebooks-linux-setup/linux-backup-restore-chromebook/\" data-orig-file=\"https://chromeunboxed.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/08/linux-backup-restore-chromebook.jpg\" data-orig-size=\"1600,900\" data-comments-opened=\"1\" data-image-meta=\"{&#34;aperture&#34;:&#34;0&#34;,&#34;credit&#34;:&#34;&#34;,&#34;camera&#34;:&#34;&#34;,&#34;caption&#34;:&#34;&#34;,&#34;created_timestamp&#34;:&#34;0&#34;,&#34;copyright&#34;:&#34;&#34;,&#34;focal_length&#34;:&#34;0&#34;,&#34;iso&#34;:&#34;0&#34;,&#34;shutter_speed&#34;:&#34;0&#34;,&#34;title&#34;:&#34;&#34;,&#34;orientation&#34;:&#34;0&#34;}\" data-image-title=\"linux-backup-restore-chromebook\" data-image-description=\"\" data-medium-file=\"https://chromeunboxed.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/08/linux-backup-restore-chromebook-300x169.jpg\" data-large-file=\"https://chromeunboxed.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/08/linux-backup-restore-chromebook-1024x576.jpg\" /></a>\n<p>If you&#8217;ve been exploring Linux apps on Chrome OS with us, it&#8217;s high time we covered how to save your current setup so you can restore it in a pinch. Full Linux distros have various tools available to run backups and store them locally or off site depending on your personal preference. Chrome OS, however, has a built in tool for this task and you can keep you backup right on you Chromebook or better yet, put it on Google Drive so it&#8217;s safe. This is very handy if you run into a situation where you need to<a rel=\"noreferrer noopener\" href=\"https://chromeunboxed.com/chromebook-chrome-os-factory-reset-how-to-powerwash/\" data-type=\"post\" data-id=\"19234\" target=\"_blank\"> power wash your device or heaven forbid have a system crash that requires a full recovery. </a></p>\n\n\n\n<p>To get started, it&#8217;s a good idea to make sure your Linux container is up to date. You can do this by opening your Linux terminal and pasting the following command. Press enter and wait for any upgrades to complete. Once that&#8217;s finished, head over to your Chrome OS settings by clicking the gear icon in the system tray at the bottom right of the display. (It&#8217;s where the clock lives.)</p>\n\n\n\n<pre class=\"wp-block-code\"><code>sudo apt update &#38;&#38; sudo apt upgrade -y</code></pre>\n\n\n\n<h2>Backup</h2>\n\n\n\n<p>In the settings, you should see Linux (Beta) in the left-hand menu. Click that and click the arrow on the right side of the Linux tab. This will open the Linux menu where you should see a tab that says &#8220;Backup &#38; restore.&#8221; Click that tab. We&#8217;re going to run a backup so click that option. When the file picker opens, you can set a custom filename for your backup and save it in the Files folder but I recommend selecting My Drive. This will save the backup to you Google Drive and you&#8217;ll be able to access it anywhere on any device. Once you&#8217;ve chosen your folder, click save an you will see the progress of the backup in the notification tray of your Chromebook. Now you have a full backup of all your Linux apps and associated data. </p>\n\n\n\n<h2>Restore</h2>\n\n\n\n<p>To restore a previous backup, we will return to the Linux settings menu and select &#8220;restore&#8221; instead of backup. Once the file picker opens, navigate to the folder with the backup you wish to use and select it. Click &#8220;open&#8221; to initiate the restore but keep in mind, this will delete any apps or data you have in your current Linux container. You will once again see a progress bar in the notification tray and when it is finished, your previously backed up Linux setup will be ready to use on your device. Simply open the Linux terminal and type a command to open a package or head to the app launcher to find apps that you have installed. That&#8217;s it for today. Stay tuned for more tips from The Command Line. </p>\n\n\n\n<div class=\"newsletterSignupBlock\">\n\tGet Alerted About New Posts On <br class=\"forMobileOnly\"><span class=\"highlighter\">Your This happens to be one of those deals. </p>\n\n\n\n<p>Outside of the Pixel Slate, the Acer Chromebook 715 was one of the first models to hit the market with a built-in fingerprint sensor. The 15.6&#8243; aluminum Chromebook is powered by 8th Gen Intel processors and features an integrated numeric keypad. Woot! is currently offering refurbished Core i3 or Core i5 versions of the Chromebook 715 at a price that&#8217;s significantly lower than a new model. Again, we shy away from renewed devices but in today&#8217;s market, we&#8217;re on the lookout for any and all Chromebooks that are worth buying. It&#8217;s very difficult to find premium devices at all let alone at a decent price. That nabs you a powerful 15.6&#8243; FullHD Chromebook with a lot of bells and whistles for just under $520 and that&#8217;s a tough deal to beat in the current climate. If you want more horsepower, you can pick up the Core i5 with a whopping 16GB of RAM for $669.99. data-large-file=\"https://chromeunboxed.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/09/chromeOS85externalDisplayRefreshRate-1024x576.jpg\" /></a>\n<p>It&#8217;s been quite some time since the <a href=\"https://chromeunboxed.com/chromebook-smoother-screen-refresh-ipad-pro-promotion/\" data-type=\"post\" data-id=\"10368\">initial experimental flags for Chromebooks showed up that allowed for higher refresh rates on external monitors.</a>  Prior to this change, Chrome OS was great at extending displays in all sorts of resolutions, but not all sorts of frame rates.  In Chrome OS 85, however, those flags necessary to make these higher refresh rates happen are in the past and the new normal sees users able to connect up their external monitors that support higher refresh rates and fully take advantage of them.</p>\n\n\n\n<p>Just today, we hooked up a Chromebook to a monitor in the office and realized that it actually supports more than 60hz and actually has the ability to leverage 75hz.  To be honest, we didn&#8217;t even know this particular monitor had that ability at all, but Chrome OS&#8217; new display settings make finding and changing the refresh rates on your connected monitors a snap.  <strong>Here&#8217;s how to make the changes and see if your monitor is capable of smoother refresh rates.</strong></p>\n\n\n\n<div class=\"wp-block-image\"><figure class=\"aligncenter size-large is-resized\"><img data-attachment-id=\"25235\" data-permalink=\"https://chromeunboxed.com/chrome-os-85-refresh-rates-selector-smooth-external-display/chromeos85settingsdisplay/\" 768w, https://chromeunboxed.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/09/ChromeOS85SettingsDisplay.png 970w\" sizes=\"(max-width: 493px) 100vw, 493px\" /></figure></div>\n\n\n\n<p>Navigate to your settings and head to <strong>Device &#62; Displays &#62; Select your display &#62; Refresh Rate</strong> and you&#8217;ll now have the option to choose all the possible refresh rates that your monitor supports.  Sadly, my daily driver display &#8211; an LG ultrawide 3440&#215;1440 &#8211; doesn&#8217;t support anything higher than 60hz.  After seeing Chrome OS running at 75hz on Gabriel&#8217;s monitor, I have to admit I&#8217;m already considering an upgrade.  There&#8217;s something about that fluid, buttery motion on higher refresh screens that <strong><em>looks so awesome.</em></strong>  If you&#8217;ve never seen it in action, you may want to go and hit up your settings to see if your connected display has the ability to push a better refresh rate.  <strong>Trust me, if it does, your eyes will thank you.</strong></p>\n\n\n\n<img src=\"https://chromeunboxed.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/09/patreonInContentDesktop.jpg\" class=\"patreonDesktop\">\n\n\n\n<div class=\"newsletterSignupBlock\">\n\tGet Alerted About New Posts On <br +0000","feedId":13345,"bgimg":"https://chromeunboxed.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/09/chromeOS85externalDisplayRefreshRate-1024x576.jpg","linkMd5":"2b41ee3eb1e098f01049b421c4510e63","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx33/cdn25@2020_6/2020/09/05/08-50-11-432_f842974c2982b134.webp","destWidth":1024,"destHeight":576,"sourceBytes":101164,"destBytes":68424,"author":"Robby Payne","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://chromeunboxed.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/09/chromeOS85externalDisplayRefreshRate-1024x576.jpg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx33/cdn25@2020_6/2020/09/05/08-50-11-432_f842974c2982b134.webp","https://chromeunboxed.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/09/ChromeOS85SettingsDisplay-927x1024.png":null,"https://chromeunboxed.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/09/patreonInContentDesktop.jpg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx5oss/cdn34@2020_5/2020/09/05/08-50-16-094_3e3fd54965f36783.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1599295810954},{"createdTime":"2020-09-05 16:50:10","updatedTime":"2020-09-05 16:50:10","title":"3 ways to install Linux packages on your Chromebook","link":"https://chromeunboxed.com/?p=24997","description":"<a href=\"https://chromeunboxed.com/3-ways-to-install-linux-packages-on-your-chromebook/\" title=\"3 ways to install Linux packages on your Chromebook\" rel=\"nofollow\"><img width=\"1024\" height=\"576\" src=\"https://chromeunboxed.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/08/install-deb-package-how-to-1024x576.jpg\" class=\"webfeedsFeaturedVisual wp-post-image\" alt=\"\" loading=\"lazy\" style=\"display: block; margin: auto; margin-bottom: 10px;max-width: 100%;\" link_thumbnail=\"1\" srcset=\"https://chromeunboxed.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/08/install-deb-package-how-to-1024x576.jpg 1024w, https://chromeunboxed.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/08/install-deb-package-how-to-300x169.jpg 300w, https://chromeunboxed.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/08/install-deb-package-how-to-768x432.jpg 768w, https://chromeunboxed.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/08/install-deb-package-how-to-1536x864.jpg 1536w, https://chromeunboxed.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/08/install-deb-package-how-to.jpg 1600w\" sizes=\"(max-width: 1024px) 100vw, 1024px\" data-attachment-id=\"24999\" data-permalink=\"https://chromeunboxed.com/3-ways-to-install-linux-packages-on-your-chromebook/install-deb-package-how-to/\" data-orig-file=\"https://chromeunboxed.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/08/install-deb-package-how-to.jpg\" data-orig-size=\"1600,900\" data-comments-opened=\"1\" data-image-meta=\"{&#34;aperture&#34;:&#34;0&#34;,&#34;credit&#34;:&#34;&#34;,&#34;camera&#34;:&#34;&#34;,&#34;caption&#34;:&#34;&#34;,&#34;created_timestamp&#34;:&#34;0&#34;,&#34;copyright&#34;:&#34;&#34;,&#34;focal_length&#34;:&#34;0&#34;,&#34;iso&#34;:&#34;0&#34;,&#34;shutter_speed&#34;:&#34;0&#34;,&#34;title&#34;:&#34;&#34;,&#34;orientation&#34;:&#34;0&#34;}\" data-image-title=\"install-deb-package-how-to\" data-image-description=\"\" data-medium-file=\"https://chromeunboxed.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/08/install-deb-package-how-to-300x169.jpg\" data-large-file=\"https://chromeunboxed.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/08/install-deb-package-how-to-1024x576.jpg\" /></a>\n<p>If you&#8217;re new to Chrome OS or maybe you&#8217;re just learning your way around the recent addition of Linux apps, you may have found yourself a bit overwhelmed with it all. You&#8217;ve probably spent precious time searching the web to figure out which Linux apps are available via your shiny, new Linux terminal. Speaking of the terminal, it&#8217;s completely understandable if you&#8217;ve slammed your Chromebook shut after a few hours of trying to grasp the Linux lingo and how to use the command line interface. Well, that&#8217;s why we&#8217;re here. The Command Line series was created to explore Linux on Chrome OS and help you make the most out of your device. We aim to demystify the newly found operating system inside your operating system. </p>\n\n\n\n<p>Today, we&#8217;re going to take a look a three different methods for installing applications, or packages in the Linux container on Chrome OS. If you&#8217;ve downloaded a .deb file from a trusted source, most often you can simply double-click the file and the Linux installer will take over and install the package for you. However, learning your way around the terminal and the various methods by which you can install Linux packages can be very useful for a number of reasons. First, many of the packages regularly used in Linux are available directly from the Debian 10 (Buster) repository and you can download and install them from the terminal with a single command. Second and equally as useful, some command line tools will handle installing dependencies that don&#8217;t come along for the ride when you install a Linux package. Later, we&#8217;ll explore various package managers and building apps from the source. For now, let&#8217;s just look at these three terminal commands. </p>\n\n\n<div class=\"crp_related crp_related_block  \"><h5>more from Chrome Unboxed</h5><ul><li><a href=\"https://chromeunboxed.com/the-command-line-getting-started-with-linux-apps-on-your-chromebook/\"    target=\"_blank\"  class=\"post-15431\"><img loading=\"lazy\"  width=\"150\" height=\"150\"  src=\"https://chromeunboxed.com/wp-content/uploads/2018/04/AdaptaLinuxChromebook-150x150.png\" class=\"crp_thumb crp_featured\" alt=\"The Command Line: Getting started with Linux apps on your Chromebook\" title=\"The Command Line: Getting started with Linux apps on your Chromebook\" srcset=\"https://chromeunboxed.com/wp-content/uploads/2018/04/AdaptaLinuxChromebook-150x150.png 150w, https://chromeunboxed.com/wp-content/uploads/2018/04/AdaptaLinuxChromebook-350x350.png 350w\" sizes=\"(max-width: 150px) 100vw, 150px\" /><span class=\"crp_title\">The Command Line: Getting started with Linux apps on your Chromebook</span></a><span class=\"crp_author\">  by <a href=\"https://chromeunboxed.com/author/gabe/\">Gabriel Brangers</a></span> </li></ul><div class=\"crp_clear\"></div></div>\n\n\n<h2>dpkg</h2>\n\n\n\n<p><code>dpkg</code> (Debian Package) is the base package management system that is built into the Debian operating system. If you have a locally saved .deb package, you  can use <code>dpkg</code> to install it. <code>dpkg</code> has some other functions but we&#8217;ll cover those in another article. To install a .deb package with <code>dpkg</code>, open up your Linux terminal to get started. The basic installation command is <code>dpkg -i some.deb</code>. and you will replace the some.deb with the exact file name that you are installing. This only works for local files and you will need to specify the path to the file. If you move your .deb package to the Linux folder in the Chrome OS files app, you don&#8217;t have to edit the command in any way. While dpkg is a sure-fire way to install locally stored packages, it&#8217;s not the go-to method for most Linux users. The more common tool for installation is <strong>APT</strong>.</p>\n\n\n\n<h2>APT</h2>\n\n\n\n<p><strong>APT</strong> is exactly what it stands for, Advanced Package Tool. It is a tool that builds on the <code>dpkg</code> package management system. Unlike <code>dpkg</code>, <strong>APT</strong> has the ability to fetch .deb packages from whatever repositories are present in your Linux OS. For Chromebooks, the repo is Buster Main and Google&#8217;s in-house repository specifically for Chrome OS. When you use <strong>APT</strong>, your requested packaged will be downloaded and installed in once action. Using APT in the Chrome OS Linux terminal also requires super user permissions. Therefore, it is used with the <code>sudo</code> command. For this example, we&#8217;ll install a random game I found called Blob and Conquer. You would install this package using APT with the following command. Once installed, you can launch the app by typing the package name in the terminal and hitting enter or finding the app icon in your app launcher.</p>\n\n\n\n<pre class=\"wp-block-code\"><code>sudo apt install blobandconquer</code></pre>\n\n\n\n<p>To install from the repository, you will need to know exactly how the package is named in the repo. To find the full list of packages available in Debian Buster, you can check out the official list <a rel=\"noreferrer noopener\" href=\"https://packages.debian.org/stable/allpackages\" target=\"_blank\">here</a>. <strong>APT</strong> can also install local packages with same command. Instead of the app name, you would use the filename just like we did with <code>dpkg</code>. If you have a .deb file in you Linux folder, simply use the following command to install it but replace the last part with the actual filename to include the .deb extension. </p>\n\n\n\n<pre class=\"wp-block-code\"><code>sudo apt install ./some.deb</code></pre>\n\n\n\n<h2>GDebi</h2>\n\n\n\n<p>Last but not least, we have GDebi. This tool isn&#8217;t built into the Debian container on Chrome OS but you can install it directly from the repository using the APT command we just discussed. GDebi is another tool that can be used to install locally saved .deb files. That advantage to using GDebi instead of dpkg or APT for local files is that GDepi will ping the repository and pull down any necessary dependencies needed to install the locally saved .dep package. There are a lot of packages that require additional downloads to work properly and this tool will ensure you have all the right pieces to install your application. You can install GDebi with the following command. </p>\n\n\n\n<pre class=\"wp-block-code\"><code>sudo apt install gdebi</code></pre>\n\n\n\n<p>GDebi does come with a user interface that can be launched from your Chrome OS app drawer but I have found it to be unreliable on my device. Instead, you can use GDebi from the terminal in combination with the <code>sudo</code> command. Once you have a .deb file downloaded and moved to your Linux folder, you  can install it and any dependencies using GDebi with the following command. Make sure to replace the last portion with the actual filename. </p>\n\n\n\n<pre class=\"wp-block-code\"><code>sudo gdebi somepackage.deb</code></pre>\n\n\n\n<p>So, there you go. You&#8217;re now on your way to mastering three more tools in your Linux arsenal. Check back tomorrow when we will cover two very different package managers that can make installing packages a breeze. We&#8217;d love to hear from you. If you&#8217;re exploring Linux apps on Chrome OS, what obstacles have you run into? What tips would help you feel more confident inside the terminal? Drop a comment or shoot us an email and we&#8217;ll get it on the &#8220;how-to&#8221;  list. Stay tuned. There&#8217;s more Command Line headed your way. </p>\n\n\n\n<div class=\"newsletterSignupBlock\">\n\tGet Alerted About New Posts On <br class=\"forMobileOnly\"><span class=\"highlighter\">Your
